	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Natural law</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">System of law that is purportedly determined by nature, and is thus universal</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Natural_law_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Natural law (disambiguation)">Natural law (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carlo_Crivelli_007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Carlo_Crivelli_007.jpg/200px-Carlo_Crivelli_007.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Carlo_Crivelli_007.jpg/300px-Carlo_Crivelli_007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Carlo_Crivelli_007.jpg/400px-Carlo_Crivelli_007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2024" data-file-height="3144" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carlo_Crivelli_007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Catholic_philosopher" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic philosopher">Catholic philosopher</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, revived and developed the concept of natural law from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">ancient Greek philosophy</a></div></div></div>
<p><b>Natural law</b> (<a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">ius naturale</i>, <i>lex naturalis</i>) is law that is held to exist independently of the positive law of a given political order, society or nation-state. As determined by nature, the <b>law of nature</b> is implied to be objective and universal;<sup id="cite_ref-IESS_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IESS-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> it exists independently of human understanding, and of the <a href="/wiki/Positive_law" title="Positive law">positive law</a> of a given <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">state</a>, <a href="/wiki/Political_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Political order">political order</a>, <a href="/wiki/Legislature" title="Legislature">legislature</a> or <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">society</a> at large. Historically, natural law refers to the use of reason to analyze <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a> to <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deduce</a> binding rules of <a href="/wiki/Moral_behavior" class="mw-redirect" title="Moral behavior">moral behavior</a> from nature's or God's creation of <a href="/wiki/Reality" title="Reality">reality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">mankind</a>.
</p><p>The concept of natural law was documented in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">ancient Greek philosophy</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> and was referred to in <a href="/wiki/Roman_philosophy" title="Roman philosophy">Roman philosophy</a> by <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>. References to natural law are also found in the Old and New Testaments of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, later expounded upon in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> by <a href="/wiki/Christian_philosopher" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian philosopher">Christian philosophers</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Albert_the_Great" class="mw-redirect" title="Albert the Great">Albert the Great</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>. The <a href="/wiki/School_of_Salamanca" title="School of Salamanca">School of Salamanca</a> made notable contributions during the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>.
</p><p>Modern natural law theories were greatly developed in the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>, combining inspiration from <a href="/wiki/Roman_law" title="Roman law">Roman law</a> with philosophies like <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a> theory. It was used to challenge the <a href="/wiki/Divine_right_of_kings" title="Divine right of kings">divine right of kings</a>, and became an alternative justification for the establishment of a <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">social contract</a>, <a href="/wiki/Positive_law" title="Positive law">positive law</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a>—and thus legal rights—in the form of <a href="/wiki/Classical_republicanism" title="Classical republicanism">classical republicanism</a>. Conversely, the concept of natural rights is used by others to challenge the legitimacy of all such establishments.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Contemporarily, the concept of natural law is closely related to the concept of <a href="/wiki/Natural_and_legal_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural and legal rights">natural rights</a>. Indeed, many <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosophers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jurist" title="Jurist">jurists</a> and scholars use natural law synonymously with natural rights (<a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Ius_naturale" title="Ius naturale">ius naturale</a></i>), or <a href="/wiki/Natural_justice" title="Natural justice">natural justice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shellens_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shellens-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> while others distinguish between natural law and natural right.<sup id="cite_ref-IESS_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IESS-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the intersection between natural law and natural rights, natural law has been claimed or attributed as a key component in the <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a></i> (1776) of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen" title="Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen">Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen</a></i> (1789) of <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a></i> (1948) of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations</a>, as well as the <i><a href="/wiki/European_Convention_on_Human_Rights" title="European Convention on Human Rights">European Convention on Human Rights</a></i> (1953) of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Greece">Ancient Greece</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Ancient Greece">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plato">Plato</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Plato">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="box-Original_research plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Original_research" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>possibly contains <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research">original research</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit">improve it</a> by <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verifying</a> the claims made and adding <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Inline_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">inline citations</a>. Statements consisting only of original research should be removed.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">May 2011</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table><p>Although <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> did not have an explicit theory of natural law (he rarely used the phrase 'natural law' except in <i><a href="/wiki/Gorgias_(dialogue)" title="Gorgias (dialogue)">Gorgias</a></i> 484 and <i><a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(dialogue)" title="Timaeus (dialogue)">Timaeus</a></i> 83e), his concept of nature, according to <a href="/wiki/John_Daniel_Wild" title="John Daniel Wild">John Wild</a>, contains some of the elements found in many natural law theories.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> According to Plato, we live in an orderly universe.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The basis of this orderly universe or nature are the <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_forms" title="Theory of forms">forms</a>, most fundamentally the <a href="/wiki/Form_of_the_Good" title="Form of the Good">Form of the Good</a>, which Plato describes as "the brightest region of Being".<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The Form of the Good is the cause of all things, and when it is seen it leads a person to act wisely.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In the <i><a href="/wiki/Symposium_(Plato)" title="Symposium (Plato)">Symposium</a></i>, the Good is closely identified with the Beautiful.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In the <i>Symposium</i>, Plato describes how the experience of the Beautiful by <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> enabled him to resist the temptations of wealth and sex.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In the <i><a href="/wiki/Republic_(Plato)" title="Republic (Plato)">Republic</a></i>, the ideal community is "a city which would be established in accordance with nature".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Aristotle">Aristotle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Aristotle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Treatise_on_Law#Natural_law" title="Treatise on Law">Treatise on Law §&#160;Natural law</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg/170px-Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg/255px-Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg/340px-Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="804" data-file-height="1052" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plato (left) and Aristotle (right), a detail of <i><a href="/wiki/The_School_of_Athens" title="The School of Athens">The School of Athens</a></i>, a fresco by <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek philosophy">Greek philosophy</a> emphasized the distinction between "nature" (<i>physis</i>, <i>φúσις</i>) on the one hand and "law", "custom", or "<a href="/wiki/Convention_(norm)" title="Convention (norm)">convention</a>" (<i>nomos</i>, <i>νóμος</i>) on the other.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> What the law commanded would be expected to vary from place to place, but what was "by nature" should be the same everywhere. A "law of nature" would therefore have the flavor more of a paradox than something that obviously existed.<sup id="cite_ref-IESS_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IESS-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Against the <a href="/wiki/Conventionalism" title="Conventionalism">conventionalism</a> that the distinction between nature and custom could engender, Socrates and his philosophic heirs, Plato and <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, posited the existence of natural justice or natural right (<i>dikaion physikon</i>, <i>δικαιον φυσικον</i>, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>ius naturale</i>). Of these, Aristotle is often said to be the father of natural law.<sup id="cite_ref-Shellens_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shellens-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Aristotle's association with natural law may be due to the interpretation given to his works by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Jaffa_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jaffa-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> But whether Aquinas correctly read Aristotle is in dispute. According to some, Aquinas conflates natural law and natural right, the latter of which Aristotle posits in Book V of the <i><a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a></i> (Book IV of the <i><a href="/wiki/Eudemian_Ethics" title="Eudemian Ethics">Eudemian Ethics</a></i>). According to this interpretation, Aquinas's influence was such as to affect a number of early translations of these passages in an unfortunate manner, though more recent translations render those more literally.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Aristotle notes that <a href="/wiki/Natural_justice" title="Natural justice">natural justice</a> is a species of political justice, specifically the scheme of <a href="/wiki/Distributive_justice" title="Distributive justice">distributive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Restorative_justice" title="Restorative justice">corrective justice</a> that would be established under the best political community; were this to take the form of law, this could be called a natural law, though Aristotle does not discuss this and suggests in the <i><a href="/wiki/Politics_(Aristotle)" title="Politics (Aristotle)">Politics</a></i> that the best regime may not rule by law at all.<sup id="cite_ref-Corbett_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corbett-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The best evidence of Aristotle's having thought there was a natural law comes from the <i><a href="/wiki/Rhetoric_(Aristotle)" title="Rhetoric (Aristotle)">Rhetoric</a></i>, where Aristotle notes that, aside from the "particular" laws that each people has set up for itself, there is a "common" law that is according to nature.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Specifically, he quotes Sophocles and Empedocles:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Universal law is the law of Nature. For there really is, as every one to some extent divines, a natural justice and injustice that is binding on all men, even on those who have no association or covenant with each other. It is this that Sophocles' Antigone clearly means when she says that the burial of Polyneices was a just act in spite of the prohibition: she means that it was just by nature:
</p>
<dl><dd>"Not of to-day or yesterday it is,</dd>
<dd>But lives eternal: none can date its birth."</dd></dl>
<p>And so Empedocles, when he bids us kill no living creature, he is saying that to do this is not just for some people, while unjust for others:
</p>
<dl><dd>"Nay, but, an all-embracing law, through the realms of the sky</dd>
<dd>Unbroken it stretcheth, and over the earth's immensity."<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
</blockquote>
<p>Some critics believe that the context of this remark suggests only that Aristotle advised that it could be rhetorically advantageous to appeal to such a law, especially when the "particular" law of one's own city was averse to the case being made, not that there actually was such a law;<sup id="cite_ref-Shellens_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shellens-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Moreover, they claim that Aristotle considered two of the three candidates for a universally valid, natural law provided in this passage to be wrong.<sup id="cite_ref-IESS_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IESS-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Aristotle's paternity of natural law tradition is consequently disputed.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stoic_natural_law">Stoic natural law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Stoic natural law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The development of this tradition of <a href="/wiki/Natural_justice" title="Natural justice">natural justice</a> into one of natural law is usually attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoics</a>. The rise of natural law as a universal system coincided with the rise of large empires and kingdoms in the Greek world.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed (November 2012)">full citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Whereas the "higher" law that Aristotle suggested one could appeal to was emphatically <a href="/wiki/Appeal_to_nature" title="Appeal to nature">natural</a>, in contradistinction to being the result of <a href="/wiki/Divinity" title="Divinity">divine</a> <a href="/wiki/Positive_law" title="Positive law">positive</a> <a href="/wiki/Legislation" title="Legislation">legislation</a>, the Stoic natural law was indifferent to either the natural or divine source of the law: the Stoics asserted the existence of a rational and purposeful order to the universe (a <a href="/wiki/Divine_law" title="Divine law">divine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Eternal_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Eternal law">eternal law</a>), and the means by which a rational being lived in accordance with this order was the natural law, which inspired actions that accorded with virtue.<sup id="cite_ref-IESS_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IESS-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As the English historian A. J. Carlyle (1861–1943) notes:
</p>
<blockquote>
<p>There is no change in political theory so startling in its completeness as the change from the theory of Aristotle to the later philosophical view represented by Cicero and Seneca&#160;... We think that this cannot be better exemplified than with regard to the theory of the equality of human nature."<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Charles H. McIlwain likewise observes that "the idea of the equality of men is the most profound contribution of the Stoics to political thought" and that "its greatest influence is in the changed conception of law that in part resulted from it.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Natural law first appeared among the stoics who believed that God is everywhere and in everyone (see <a href="/wiki/Classical_pantheism" title="Classical pantheism">classical pantheism</a>). According to this belief, within humans there is a "divine spark" which helps them to live in accordance with nature. The stoics felt that there was a way in which the universe had been designed, and that natural law helped us to harmonise with this.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Rome">Ancient Rome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ancient Rome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thorvaldsen_Cicero.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Thorvaldsen_Cicero.jpg/180px-Thorvaldsen_Cicero.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Thorvaldsen_Cicero.jpg/270px-Thorvaldsen_Cicero.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Thorvaldsen_Cicero.jpg/360px-Thorvaldsen_Cicero.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2174" data-file-height="3008" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thorvaldsen_Cicero.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Marcus_Tullius_Cicero" class="mw-redirect" title="Marcus Tullius Cicero">Marcus Tullius Cicero</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> wrote in his <a href="/wiki/De_Legibus" title="De Legibus">De Legibus</a> that both justice and law originate from what nature has given to humanity, from what the human mind embraces, from the function of humanity, and from what serves to unite humanity.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> For Cicero, natural law obliges us to contribute to the general good of the larger society.<sup id="cite_ref-Barham_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barham-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The purpose of positive laws is to provide for "the safety of citizens, the preservation of states, and the tranquility and happiness of human life." In this view, "wicked and unjust statutes" are "anything but 'laws,'" because "in the very definition of the term 'law' there <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/inheres" class="extiw" title="wikt:inheres">inheres</a> the idea and principle of choosing what is just and true."<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Law, for Cicero, "ought to be a reformer of vice and an incentive to virtue."<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Cicero expressed the view that "the virtues which we ought to cultivate, always tend to our own happiness, and that the best means of promoting them consists in living with men in that perfect union and charity which are cemented by mutual benefits."<sup id="cite_ref-Barham_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barham-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In De Re Publica, he writes:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There is indeed a law, right reason, which is in accordance with nature&#160;; existing in all, unchangeable, eternal. Commanding us to do what is right, forbidding us to do what is wrong. It has dominion over good men, but possesses no influence over bad ones. No other law can be substituted for it, no part of it can be taken away, nor can it be abrogated altogether. Neither the people or the senate can absolve from it. It is not one thing at Rome, and another thing at Athens&#160;: one thing to-day, and another thing to-morrow&#160;; but it is eternal and immutable for all nations and for all time.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Cicero influenced the discussion of natural law for many centuries to come, up through the era of the American Revolution. The jurisprudence of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> was rooted in Cicero, who held "an extraordinary grip ... upon the imagination of posterity" as "the medium for the propagation of those ideas which informed the law and institutions of the empire."<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Cicero's conception of natural law "found its way to later centuries notably through the writings of <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Seville" title="Isidore of Seville">Saint Isidore of Seville</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Decretum_Gratiani" title="Decretum Gratiani">Decretum of Gratian</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>, in his summary of medieval natural law, quoted Cicero's statement that "nature" and "custom" were the sources of a society's laws.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Renaissance Italian historian <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Bruni" title="Leonardo Bruni">Leonardo Bruni</a> praised Cicero as the person "who carried philosophy from Greece to Italy, and nourished it with the golden river of his eloquence."<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The legal culture of Elizabethan England, exemplified by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Coke" title="Edward Coke">Sir Edward Coke</a>, was "steeped in Ciceronian rhetoric."<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The Scottish moral philosopher <a href="/wiki/Francis_Hutcheson_(philosopher)" title="Francis Hutcheson (philosopher)">Francis Hutcheson</a>, as a student at Glasgow, "was attracted most by Cicero, for whom he always professed the greatest admiration."<sup id="cite_ref-WRScott_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WRScott-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> More generally in eighteenth-century Great Britain, Cicero's name was a household word among educated people.<sup id="cite_ref-WRScott_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WRScott-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Likewise, "in the admiration of early Americans Cicero took pride of place as orator, political theorist, stylist, and moralist."<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The British polemicist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Gordon_(writer)" title="Thomas Gordon (writer)">Thomas Gordon</a> "incorporated Cicero into the radical ideological tradition that travelled from the mother country to the colonies in the course of the eighteenth century and decisively shaped early American political culture."<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Cicero's description of the immutable, eternal, and universal natural law was quoted by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Burlamaqui" title="Jean-Jacques Burlamaqui">Burlamaqui</a><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> and later by the American revolutionary legal scholar <a href="/wiki/James_Wilson" title="James Wilson">James Wilson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Cicero became <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>'s "foremost model of public service, republican virtue, and forensic eloquence."<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Adams wrote of Cicero that "as all the ages of the world have not produced a greater statesman and philosopher united in the same character, his authority should have great weight."<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> "first encountered Cicero as a schoolboy while learning Latin, and continued to read his letters and discourses throughout his life. He admired him as a patriot, valued his opinions as a moral philosopher, and there is little doubt that he looked upon Cicero's life, with his love of study and aristocratic country life, as a model for his own."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Jefferson described Cicero as "the father of eloquence and philosophy."<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The New Testament carries a further exposition on the Abrahamic dialogue and links to the later Greek exposition on the subject, when <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul of Tarsus">Paul</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Romans" title="Epistle to the Romans">Epistle to the Romans</a> states: "For when the Gentiles, which have not the law, do by nature the things contained in the law, these, having not the law, are a law unto themselves: Which shew the work of the law written in their hearts, their conscience also bearing witness, and their thoughts the meanwhile accusing or else excusing one another."<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The intellectual historian A. J. Carlyle has commented on this passage, "There can be little doubt that St Paul's words imply some conception analogous to the 'natural law' in <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, a law written in men's hearts, recognized by man's reason, a law distinct from the positive law of any State, or from what St Paul recognized as <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">the revealed law of God</a>. It is in this sense that St Paul's words are taken by the Fathers of the fourth and fifth centuries like <a href="/wiki/Hilary_of_Poitiers" title="Hilary of Poitiers">St Hilary of Poitiers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ambrose" title="Ambrose">St Ambrose</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">St Augustine</a>, and there seems no reason to doubt the correctness of their interpretation."<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of its origins in the Old Testament, early <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a>, especially those in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Christianity" title="Western Christianity">West</a>, saw natural law as part of the natural foundation of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>. The most notable among these was <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>, who equated natural law with humanity's <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_man" title="Fall of man">prelapsarian</a> state; as such, a life according to unbroken human nature was no longer possible and persons needed instead to seek healing and salvation through the <a href="/wiki/Divine_law" title="Divine law">divine law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Divine_grace" title="Divine grace">grace</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a>.
</p><p>The natural law was inherently <a href="/wiki/Teleological" class="mw-redirect" title="Teleological">teleological</a>, however, it is most assuredly not <a href="/wiki/Deontological" class="mw-redirect" title="Deontological">deontological</a>. For Christians, natural law is how human beings manifest the divine image in their life. This mimicry of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">God</a>'s own life is impossible to accomplish except by means of the power of grace. Thus, whereas deontological systems merely require certain duties be performed, Christianity explicitly states that no one can, in fact, perform any duties if grace is lacking. For Christians, natural law flows not from divine commands, but from the fact that humanity is made in God's image, humanity is empowered by God's grace. Living the natural law is how humanity displays the gifts of life and grace, the gifts of all that is good. Consequences are in God's hands, consequences are generally not within human control, thus in natural law, actions are judged by three things: (1) the person's intent, (2) the circumstances of the act and (3) the nature of the act. The apparent good or evil consequence resulting from the moral act is not relevant to the act itself. The specific content of the natural law is therefore determined by how each person's acts mirror God's internal life of love. Insofar as one lives the natural law, temporal satisfaction may or may not be attained, but salvation will be attained. The <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a>, in being bound by the natural law, is conceived as an institution whose purpose is to assist in bringing its subjects to true happiness. True happiness derives from living in harmony with the mind of God as an image of the living God.
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, some <a href="/wiki/Protestant_denominations" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant denominations">Protestant denominations</a> maintained parts of the Catholic concept of natural law. The <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">English</a> <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theologian</a> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hooker" title="Richard Hooker">Richard Hooker</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> adapted <a href="/wiki/Thomism" title="Thomism">Thomistic</a> notions of natural law to <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a>  five principles: to live, to learn, to reproduce, to worship God, and to live in an ordered society.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Verification of this source citation failed to find it supporting or even relevant to the material that cites it (June 2019)">irrelevant citation</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholic_natural_law_jurisprudence">Catholic natural law jurisprudence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Catholic natural law jurisprudence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Treatise_on_Law" title="Treatise on Law">Treatise on Law</a> and <a href="/wiki/Determinatio" title="Determinatio">Determinatio</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tommaso_da_modena,_ritratti_di_domenicani_(vescovo)_1352_150cm,_treviso,_ex_convento_di_san_niccol%C3%B2,_sala_del_capitolo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Tommaso_da_modena%2C_ritratti_di_domenicani_%28vescovo%29_1352_150cm%2C_treviso%2C_ex_convento_di_san_niccol%C3%B2%2C_sala_del_capitolo.jpg/220px-Tommaso_da_modena%2C_ritratti_di_domenicani_%28vescovo%29_1352_150cm%2C_treviso%2C_ex_convento_di_san_niccol%C3%B2%2C_sala_del_capitolo.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="306" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Tommaso_da_modena%2C_ritratti_di_domenicani_%28vescovo%29_1352_150cm%2C_treviso%2C_ex_convento_di_san_niccol%C3%B2%2C_sala_del_capitolo.jpg/330px-Tommaso_da_modena%2C_ritratti_di_domenicani_%28vescovo%29_1352_150cm%2C_treviso%2C_ex_convento_di_san_niccol%C3%B2%2C_sala_del_capitolo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Tommaso_da_modena%2C_ritratti_di_domenicani_%28vescovo%29_1352_150cm%2C_treviso%2C_ex_convento_di_san_niccol%C3%B2%2C_sala_del_capitolo.jpg/440px-Tommaso_da_modena%2C_ritratti_di_domenicani_%28vescovo%29_1352_150cm%2C_treviso%2C_ex_convento_di_san_niccol%C3%B2%2C_sala_del_capitolo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="679" data-file-height="944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tommaso_da_modena,_ritratti_di_domenicani_(vescovo)_1352_150cm,_treviso,_ex_convento_di_san_niccol%C3%B2,_sala_del_capitolo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">O.P.</a> (c. 1200–1280).</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Benozzo_Gozzoli_004a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Benozzo_Gozzoli_004a.jpg/190px-Benozzo_Gozzoli_004a.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Benozzo_Gozzoli_004a.jpg/285px-Benozzo_Gozzoli_004a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Benozzo_Gozzoli_004a.jpg/380px-Benozzo_Gozzoli_004a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="727" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Benozzo_Gozzoli_004a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> (1225–1274).</div></div></div>
