</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">
<p><a href="/wiki/File:046CupolaSPietro.jpg" class="image"><img alt="046CupolaSPietro.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/16px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg" decoding="async" width="16" height="12" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/24px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/046CupolaSPietro.jpg/32px-046CupolaSPietro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Portal:Catholicism">Catholicism&#32;portal</a>
</p>
<a href="/wiki/File:Socrates.png" class="image"><img alt="Socrates.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/10px-Socrates.png" decoding="async" width="10" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/15px-Socrates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/21px-Socrates.png 2x" data-file-width="326" data-file-height="500" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Philosophy" title="Portal:Philosophy">Philosophy&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Catholic_philosophy" title="Template:Catholic philosophy"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Catholic_philosophy" title="Template talk:Catholic philosophy"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Catholic_philosophy&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the twelfth century, <a href="/wiki/Gratian_(jurist)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gratian (jurist)">Gratian</a> equated the natural law with divine law. <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a> would address the subject a century later, and his pupil, St. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa Theologica</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Treatise_on_Law" title="Treatise on Law">I-II qq. 90–106</a>, restored Natural Law to its independent state, asserting natural law as the rational creature's participation in the eternal law.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Yet, since human reason could not fully comprehend the <a href="/wiki/Eternal_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Eternal law">Eternal law</a>, it needed to be supplemented by revealed <a href="/wiki/Divine_law" title="Divine law">Divine law</a>. (See also <a href="/wiki/Biblical_law_in_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Biblical law in Christianity">Biblical law in Christianity</a>.) Meanwhile, Aquinas taught that all human or positive laws were to be judged by their conformity to the natural law. An unjust law is not a law, in the full sense of the word. It retains merely the 'appearance' of law insofar as it is duly constituted and enforced in the same way a just law is, but is itself a 'perversion of law.'<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> At this point, the natural law was not only used to pass judgment on the moral worth of various laws, but also to determine what those  laws meant in the first place. This principle laid the seed for possible societal tension with reference to tyrants.<sup id="cite_ref-Burns_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burns-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> holds the view of natural law introduced by <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a> and elaborated by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> particularly in his <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Theologica" title="Summa Theologica">Summa Theologiae</a></i>, and often as filtered through the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Salamanca" title="School of Salamanca">School of Salamanca</a>. This view is also shared by some <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestants</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> and was delineated by <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> writer <a href="/wiki/C._S._Lewis" title="C. S. Lewis">C. S. Lewis</a> in his works <i><a href="/wiki/Mere_Christianity" title="Mere Christianity">Mere Christianity</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Abolition_of_Man" title="The Abolition of Man">The Abolition of Man</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></i>
</p><p>The Catholic Church understands human beings to consist of body and mind, the physical and the non-physical (or <a href="/wiki/Soul_(spirit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Soul (spirit)">soul</a> perhaps), and that the two are inextricably linked.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Humans are capable of discerning the difference between <a href="/wiki/Good_and_evil" title="Good and evil">good and evil</a> because they have a <a href="/wiki/Conscience" title="Conscience">conscience</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> There are many manifestations of the good that we can pursue. Some, like <a href="/wiki/Procreation" class="mw-redirect" title="Procreation">procreation</a>, are common to other animals, while others, like the pursuit of truth, are inclinations peculiar to the capacities of human beings.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To know what is right, one must use one's reason and apply it to Thomas Aquinas' precepts. This reason is believed to be embodied, in its most abstract form, in the concept of a primary precept: "Good is to be sought, evil avoided."<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> St. Thomas explains that:
</p>
      <blockquote>
<p>there belongs to the natural law, first, certain most general precepts, that are known to all;
      and secondly, certain secondary and more detailed precepts, which are, as it were,
      conclusions following closely from first principles. As to those general principles, the
      natural law, in the abstract, can nowise be blotted out from men's hearts. But it is blotted
      out in the case of a particular action, insofar as reason is hindered from applying the
      general principle to a particular point of practice, on account of concupiscence or some
      other passion, as stated above (77, 2). But as to the other, i.e., the secondary precepts, the
      natural law can be blotted out from the human heart, either by evil persuasions, just as in
      speculative matters errors occur in respect of necessary conclusions; or by vicious customs
      and corrupt habits, as among some men, theft, and even unnatural vices, as the Apostle
      states (Rm. i), were not esteemed sinful.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>However, while the primary and immediate precepts cannot be "blotted out", the secondary precepts can be. Therefore, for a deontological ethical theory they are open to a surprisingly large amount of interpretation and flexibility. Any rule that helps humanity to live up to the primary or subsidiary precepts can be a secondary precept, for example:
</p>
<ul><li>Drunkenness is wrong because it injures one's health, and worse, destroys one's ability to reason, which is fundamental to humans as rational animals (i.e., does not support self-preservation).</li>
<li>Theft is wrong because it destroys social relations, and humans are by nature social animals (i.e., does not support the subsidiary precept of living in society).</li></ul>
<p>Natural moral law is concerned with both exterior and interior acts, also known as action and motive. Simply doing the right thing is not enough; to be truly moral one's motive must be right as well. For example, helping an old lady across the road (good exterior act) to impress someone (bad interior act) is wrong. However, good intentions don't always lead to good actions. The motive must coincide with the cardinal or theological virtues. <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_virtues" title="Cardinal virtues">Cardinal virtues</a> are acquired through reason applied to nature; they are:
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Prudence" title="Prudence">Prudence</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Justice_(virtue)" title="Justice (virtue)">Justice</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Temperance_(virtue)" title="Temperance (virtue)">Temperance</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cardinal_virtues" title="Cardinal virtues">Fortitude</a></li></ol>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Theological_virtues" title="Theological virtues">theological virtues</a> are:
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Faith_in_Christianity" title="Faith in Christianity">Faith</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hope_(virtue)" title="Hope (virtue)">Hope</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Charity_(virtue)" title="Charity (virtue)">Charity</a></li></ol>
<p>According to Aquinas, to lack any of these virtues is to lack the ability to make a moral choice. For example, consider a person who possesses the virtues of justice, prudence, and fortitude, yet lacks temperance. Due to their lack of self-control and desire for pleasure, despite their good intentions, they will find themself swaying from the moral path.
</p><p>In the 16th century, the <a href="/wiki/School_of_Salamanca" title="School of Salamanca">School of Salamanca</a> (<a href="/wiki/Francisco_Su%C3%A1rez" title="Francisco Suárez">Francisco Suárez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Vitoria" title="Francisco de Vitoria">Francisco de Vitoria</a>, etc.) further developed a philosophy of natural law.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_natural_law">Islamic natural law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Islamic natural law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fitra" title="Fitra">Fitra</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Rayh%C4%81n_al-B%C4%ABr%C5%ABn%C4%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Abū Rayhān al-Bīrūnī">Abū Rayhān al-Bīrūnī</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Ulema" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulema">Islamic scholar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a> <a href="/wiki/Islamic_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic science">scientist</a>, understood natural law as the survival of the fittest. He argued that the <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/antagonism" class="extiw" title="wikt:antagonism">antagonism</a> between <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> beings can only be overcome through a <a href="/wiki/Divine_law" title="Divine law">divine law</a>, which he believed to have been sent through <a href="/wiki/Prophets_of_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Prophets of Islam">prophets</a>. This is also the position of the <a href="/wiki/Ashari" class="mw-redirect" title="Ashari">Ashari</a> school, the largest school of Sunni theology.<sup id="cite_ref-Corbin_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corbin-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a> (Ibn Rushd), in his treatise on <i>Justice and Jihad</i> and his commentary on Plato's <i>Republic</i>, writes that the human mind can know of the unlawfulness of killing and stealing and thus of the five <a href="/wiki/Maqasid" title="Maqasid">maqasid</a> or higher intents of the Islamic <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a> or to protect religion, life, property, offspring, and reason. The concept of natural law entered the mainstream of <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a> through his Aristotelian commentaries, influencing the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Averroism" title="Averroism">Averroist</a> movement and the writings of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Maturidi" title="Maturidi">Maturidi</a> school, the second largest school of Sunni theology, posits the existence of a form of natural law. <a href="/wiki/Abu_Mansur_al-Maturidi" title="Abu Mansur al-Maturidi">Abu Mansur al-Maturidi</a> stated that the human mind could know of the existence of God and the major forms of 'good' and 'evil' without the help of revelation. Al-Maturidi gives the example of stealing, which is known to be evil by reason alone due to people's working hard for their property. Killing, fornication, and drinking alcohol were all 'evils' the human mind could know of according to al-Maturidi. The concept of <i><a href="/wiki/Istislah" title="Istislah">Istislah</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Islamic law</a> bears some similarities to the natural law tradition in the West, as exemplified by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>. However, whereas natural law deems good what is self-evidently good, according as it tends towards the fulfilment of the person, <i>istislah</i> calls good whatever is connected to one of five "basic goods". <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al-Ghazali</a> abstracted these "basic goods" from the legal precepts in the Qur'an and Sunnah: they are religion, life, reason, lineage and property. Some add also "honour". <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Qayyim_Al-Jawziyya" class="mw-redirect" title="Ibn Qayyim Al-Jawziyya">Ibn Qayyim Al-Jawziyya</a> also posited that human reason could discern between 'great sins' and good deeds.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brehon_law">Brehon law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Brehon law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Early Irish law, An Senchus Mor (The Great Tradition) mentions in a number of places <i>recht aicned</i> or natural law. This is a concept predating European legal theory, and reflects a type of law that is universal and may be determined by reason and observation of natural action. Neil McLeod identifies concepts that law must accord with: fír (truth) and dliged (right or entitlement). These two terms occur frequently, though Irish law never strictly defines them. Similarly, the term córus (law in accordance with proper order) occurs in some places, and even in the titles of certain texts. These were two very real concepts to the jurists and the value of a given judgment with respect to them was apparently ascertainable. McLeod has also suggested that most of the specific laws mentioned have passed the test of time and thus their truth has been confirmed, while other provisions are justified in other ways because they are younger and have not been tested over time<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The laws were written in the oldest dialect of the Irish language, called Bérla Féini [Bairla-faina], which even at the time was so difficult that persons about to become brehons had to be specially instructed in it, the length of time from beginning to becoming a learned Brehon was usually 20 years. Although under the law any third person could fulfill the duty if both parties agreed, and both were sane.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> It has been included in an Ethno-Celtic breakaway subculture, as it has religious undertones and freedom of religious expression allows it to once again be used as a valid system in Western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="English_jurisprudence">English jurisprudence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: English jurisprudence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Heinrich A. Rommen remarked upon "the tenacity with which the spirit of the English common law retained the conceptions of natural law and equity which it had assimilated during the Catholic Middle Ages, thanks especially to the influence of <a href="/wiki/Henry_de_Bracton" title="Henry de Bracton">Henry de Bracton</a> (d. 1268) and <a href="/wiki/John_Fortescue_(judge)" title="John Fortescue (judge)">Sir John Fortescue</a> (d. cir. 1476)."<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Bracton's translator notes that Bracton "was a trained jurist with the principles and distinctions of Roman jurisprudence firmly in mind"; but Bracton adapted such principles to English purposes rather than copying slavishly.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In particular, Bracton turned the imperial Roman maxim that "the will of the prince is law" on its head, insisting that the king is <i>under</i> the law.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The legal historian Charles F. Mullett has noted Bracton's "ethical definition of law, his recognition of justice, and finally his devotion to natural rights."<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Bracton considered justice to be the "fountain-head" from which "all rights arise."<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> For his definition of justice, Bracton quoted the twelfth-century Italian jurist <a href="/wiki/Azo_of_Bologna" title="Azo of Bologna">Azo</a>: "'Justice is the constant and unfailing will to give to each his right.'"<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Bracton's work was the second legal treatise studied by the young apprentice lawyer <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Fortescue stressed "the supreme importance of the law of God and of nature" in works that "profoundly influenced the course of legal development in the following centuries."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The legal scholar <a href="/wiki/Ellis_Sandoz" title="Ellis Sandoz">Ellis Sandoz</a> has noted that "the historically ancient and the ontologically higher law—eternal, divine, natural—are woven together to compose a single harmonious texture in Fortescue's account of English law."<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> As the legal historian Norman Doe explains: "Fortescue follows the general pattern set by Aquinas. The objective of every legislator is to dispose people to virtue.  It is by means of law that this is accomplished. Fortescue's definition of law (also found in <a href="/wiki/Accursius" title="Accursius">Accursius</a> and Bracton), after all, was 'a sacred sanction commanding what is virtuous [<i>honesta</i>] and forbidding the contrary.'"<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Fortescue cited the great Italian <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Bruni" title="Leonardo Bruni">Leonardo Bruni</a> for his statement that "virtue alone produces happiness."<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Christopher_St._Germain" title="Christopher St. Germain">Christopher St. Germain</a>'s <i>Doctor and Student</i> was a classic of English jurisprudence,<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> and it was thoroughly annotated by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> St. Germain informs his readers that English lawyers generally don't use the phrase "law of nature", but rather use "reason" as the preferred synonym.<sup id="cite_ref-Doctor_and_Student,_bk._1,_ch._5_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doctor_and_Student,_bk._1,_ch._5-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Norman Doe notes that St. Germain's view "is essentially Thomist," quoting Thomas Aquinas's definition of law as "an ordinance of reason made for the common good by him who has charge of the community, and promulgated".<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Edward_Coke" title="Edward Coke">Sir Edward Coke</a> was the preeminent jurist of his time.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Coke's preeminence extended across the ocean: "For the American revolutionary leaders, 'law' meant Sir Edward Coke's custom and right reason."<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Coke defined law as "perfect reason, which commands those things that are proper and necessary and which prohibits contrary things".<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> For Coke, human nature determined the purpose of law; and law was superior to any one person's reason or will.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Coke's discussion of natural law appears in his report of <i>Calvin's Case</i> (1608): "The law of nature is that which God at the time of creation of the nature of man infused into his heart, for his preservation and direction." In this case the judges found that "the ligeance or faith of the subject is due unto the King by the law of nature: secondly, that the law of nature is part of the law of England: thirdly, that the law of nature was before any judicial or municipal law: fourthly, that the law of nature is immutable." To support these findings, the assembled judges (as reported by Coke, who was one of them) cited as authorities <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul of Tarsus">Apostle Paul</a>; as well as Bracton, Fortescue, and <a href="/wiki/Christopher_St._Germain" title="Christopher St. Germain">St. Germain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After Coke, the most famous common law jurist of the seventeenth century is <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Hale_(jurist)" title="Matthew Hale (jurist)">Sir Matthew Hale</a>. Hale wrote a treatise on natural law that circulated among English lawyers in the eighteenth century and survives in three manuscript copies.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> This natural-law treatise has been published as <i>Of the Law of Nature</i> (2015).<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Hale's definition of the natural law reads: "It is the Law of Almighty God given by him to Man with his Nature discovering the morall good and moral evill of Moral Actions, commanding the former, and forbidding the latter by the secret voice or dictate of his implanted nature, his reason, and his concience."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> He viewed natural law as antecedent, preparatory, and subsequent to civil government,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and stated that human law "cannot forbid what the Law of Nature injoins, nor Command what the Law of Nature prohibits."<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> He cited as authorities <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epictetus" title="Epictetus">Epictetus</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Paul_of_Tarsus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul of Tarsus">Apostle Paul</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> He was critical of Hobbes's reduction of natural law to self-preservation and Hobbes's account of the state of nature,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> but drew positively on  <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Grotius" title="Hugo Grotius">Hugo Grotius</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure_belli_ac_pacis" title="De jure belli ac pacis">De jure belli ac pacis</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Su%C3%A1rez" title="Francisco Suárez">Francisco Suárez</a>'s <i>Tractatus de legibus ac deo legislatore</i>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Selden" title="John Selden">John Selden</a>'s <i>De jure naturali et gentium juxta disciplinam Ebraeorum</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As early as the thirteenth century, it was held that "the law of nature...is the ground of all laws"<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> and by the Chancellor and Judges that "it is required by the law of nature that every person, before he can be punish'd, ought to be present; and if absent by contumacy, he ought to be summoned and make default".<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Further, in 1824, we find it held that "proceedings in our Courts are founded upon the law of England, and that law is again founded upon the law of nature and the revealed law of God. If the right sought to be enforced is inconsistent with either of these, the English municipal courts cannot recognize it."<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hobbes">Hobbes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Hobbes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Hobbes.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Thomas_Hobbes.jpeg/190px-Thomas_Hobbes.jpeg" decoding="async" width="190" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/77/Thomas_Hobbes.jpeg 1.5x" data-file-width="275" data-file-height="326" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Hobbes.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thomas Hobbes</div></div></div>
<p>By the 17th century, the <a href="/wiki/Medieval" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval">medieval</a> <a href="/wiki/Teleology" title="Teleology">teleological</a> view came under intense criticism from some quarters. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a> instead founded a <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">contractarian theory</a> of <a href="/wiki/Legal_positivism" title="Legal positivism">legal positivism</a> on what all men could agree upon: what they sought (happiness) was subject to contention, but a broad consensus could form around what they feared (violent death at the hands of another). The natural law was how a rational human being, seeking to survive and prosper, would act. Natural law, therefore, was discovered by considering humankind's <a href="/wiki/Natural_and_legal_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural and legal rights">natural rights</a>, whereas previously it could be said that natural rights were discovered by considering the natural law. In Hobbes' opinion, the only way natural law could prevail was for men to submit to the commands of the sovereign. Because the ultimate source of law now comes from the sovereign, and the sovereign's decisions need not be grounded in morality, legal positivism is born. <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham" title="Jeremy Bentham">Jeremy Bentham</a>'s modifications on <a href="/wiki/Legal_positivism" title="Legal positivism">legal positivism</a> further developed the theory.
</p><p>As used by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a> in his treatises <i><a href="/wiki/Leviathan_(Hobbes_book)" title="Leviathan (Hobbes book)">Leviathan</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/De_Cive" title="De Cive">De Cive</a></i>, natural law is "a <a href="/wiki/Precept" title="Precept">precept</a>, or general rule, found out by <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>, by which a man is forbidden to do that which is destructive of his life, or takes away the means of preserving the same; and to omit that by which he thinks it may best be preserved."<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Hobbes, there are nineteen Laws. The first two are expounded in chapter XIV of Leviathan ("of the first and second natural laws; and of contracts"); the others in chapter XV ("of other laws of nature").
</p>
<ul><li>The first law of nature is <i>that every man ought to endeavour peace, as far as he has hope of obtaining it; and when he cannot obtain it, that he may seek and use all helps and advantages of war</i>.</li>
<li>The second law of nature is <i>that a man be willing, when others are so too, as far forth, as for peace, and defence of himself he shall think it necessary, to lay down this right to all things; and be contented with so much liberty against other men, as he would allow other men against himself.</i></li>
<li>The third law is <i>that men perform their covenants made.</i> In this law of nature consisteth the fountain and original of justice... when a covenant is made, then to break it is unjust and the definition of injustice is no other than the not performance of covenant. And whatsoever is not unjust is just.</li>
<li>The fourth law is <i>that a man which receiveth benefit from another of mere grace, endeavour that he which giveth it, have no reasonable cause to repent him of his good will.</i> Breach of this law is called ingratitude.</li>
<li>The fifth law is complaisance: <i>that every man strive to accommodate himself to the rest.</i> The observers of this law may be called sociable; the contrary, stubborn, insociable, forward, intractable.</li>
<li>The sixth law is <i>that upon caution of the future time, a man ought to pardon the offences past of them that repenting, desire it.</i></li>
<li>The seventh law is <i>that in revenges, men look not at the greatness of the evil past, but the greatness of the good to follow.</i></li>
<li>The eighth law is <i>that no man by deed, word, countenance, or gesture, declare hatred or contempt of another.</i> The breach of which law is commonly called contumely.</li>
<li>The ninth law is <i>that every man acknowledge another for his equal by nature.</i> The breach of this precept is pride.</li>
<li>The tenth law is <i>that at the entrance into the conditions of peace, no man require to reserve to himself any right, which he is not content should be reserved to every one of the rest.</i> The breach of this precept is arrogance, and observers of the precept are called modest.</li>
<li>The eleventh law is that <i>if a man be trusted to judge between man and man, that he deal equally between them.</i></li>
<li>The twelfth law is <i>that such things as cannot be divided, be enjoyed in common, if it can be; and if the quantity of the thing permit, without stint; otherwise proportionably to the number of them that have right.</i></li>
<li>The thirteenth law is <i>the entire right, or else...the first possession</i> (in the case of alternating use), of a thing that <i>can neither be divided nor enjoyed in common</i> should be determined by lottery.</li>
<li>The fourteenth law is that <i>those things which cannot be enjoyed in common, nor divided, ought to be adjudged to the first possessor; and in some cases to the first born, as acquired by lot.</i></li>
<li>The fifteenth law is that <i>all men that mediate peace be allowed safe conduct.</i></li>
<li>The sixteenth law is <i>that they that are at controversie, submit their Right to the judgement of an Arbitrator.</i></li>
<li>The seventeenth law is <i>that no man is a fit Arbitrator in his own cause.</i></li>
<li>The eighteenth law is that no man should serve as a judge in a case if <i>greater profit, or honour, or pleasure apparently ariseth [for him] out of the victory of one party, than of the other.</i></li>
<li>The nineteenth law is that in a disagreement of fact, the judge should not give more weight to the testimony of one party than another, and absent other evidence, should give credit to the testimony of other witnesses.</li></ul>
<p>Hobbes's philosophy includes a frontal assault on the founding principles of the earlier natural legal tradition,<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> disregarding the traditional association of virtue with happiness,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> and likewise re-defining "law" to remove any notion of the promotion of the common good.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Hobbes has no use for <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s association of nature with human perfection, inverting Aristotle's use of the word "nature." Hobbes posits a primitive, unconnected state of nature in which men, having a "natural proclivity...to hurt each other" also have "a Right to every thing, even to one anothers body";<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> and "nothing can be Unjust" in this "warre of every man against every man" in which human life is "solitary, poore, nasty, brutish, and short."<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Rejecting <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>'s view that people join in society primarily through "a certain social spirit which nature has implanted in man,"<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Hobbes declares that men join in society simply for the purpose of "getting themselves out from that miserable condition of Warre, which is necessarily consequent...to the naturall Passions of men, when there is no visible Power to keep them in awe."<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> As part of his campaign against the classical idea of natural human sociability, Hobbes inverts that fundamental natural legal maxim, the Golden Rule. Hobbes's version is <i>"Do not that to another, which thou wouldst not have done to thy selfe."</i><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Cumberland.27s_rebuttal_of_Hobbes"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cumberland's_rebuttal_of_Hobbes">Cumberland's rebuttal of Hobbes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cumberland&#039;s rebuttal of Hobbes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The English cleric <a href="/wiki/Richard_Cumberland_(philosopher)" title="Richard Cumberland (philosopher)">Richard Cumberland</a> wrote a lengthy and influential attack on Hobbes's depiction of individual self-interest as the essential feature of human motivation. Historian Knud Haakonssen has noted that in the eighteenth century, Cumberland was commonly placed alongside <a href="/wiki/Alberico_Gentili" title="Alberico Gentili">Alberico Gentili</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Grotius" title="Hugo Grotius">Hugo Grotius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Pufendorf" class="mw-redirect" title="Samuel Pufendorf">Samuel Pufendorf</a> "in the triumvirate of seventeenth-century founders of the 'modern' school of natural law."<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The eighteenth-century philosophers <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Ashley-Cooper,_3rd_Earl_of_Shaftesbury" title="Anthony Ashley-Cooper, 3rd Earl of Shaftesbury">Shaftesbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Francis_Hutcheson_(philosopher)" title="Francis Hutcheson (philosopher)">Hutcheson</a> "were obviously inspired in part by Cumberland."<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Historian Jon Parkin likewise describes Cumberland's work as "one of the most important works of ethical and political theory of the seventeenth century."<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Parkin observes that much of Cumberland's material "is derived from Roman <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoicism</a>, particularly from the work of <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, as "Cumberland deliberately cast his engagement with Hobbes in the mould of Cicero's debate between the Stoics, who believed that nature could provide an objective morality, and <a href="/wiki/Epicureanism" title="Epicureanism">Epicureans</a>, who argued that morality was human, conventional and self-interested."<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> In doing so, Cumberland de-emphasized the overlay of Christian dogma (in particular, the doctrine of "original sin" and the corresponding presumption that humans are incapable of "perfecting" themselves without divine intervention) that had accreted to natural law in the Middle Ages.
</p><p>By way of contrast to Hobbes's multiplicity of laws, Cumberland states in the very first sentence of his <i>Treatise of the Laws of Nature</i> that "all the Laws of Nature are reduc'd to that one, of Benevolence toward all Rationals."<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> He later clarifies: "By the name <i>Rationals</i> I beg leave to understand, as well <i>God</i> as <i>Man</i>; and I do it upon the Authority of Cicero." Cumberland argues that the mature development ("perfection") of human nature involves the individual human willing and acting for the common good.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> For Cumberland, human interdependence precludes Hobbes's natural right of each individual to wage war against all the rest for personal survival. However, Haakonssen warns against reading Cumberland as a proponent of "<a href="/wiki/Enlightened_self-interest" title="Enlightened self-interest">enlightened self-interest</a>." Rather, the "proper moral love of humanity" is "a disinterested love of God through love of humanity in ourselves as well as others."<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Cumberland concludes that actions "principally conducive to our Happiness" are those that promote "the Honour and Glory of God" and also "Charity and Justice towards men."<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> Cumberland emphasizes that desiring the well-being of our fellow humans is essential to the "pursuit of our own Happiness."<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> He cites "reason" as the authority for his conclusion that happiness consists in "the most extensive Benevolence," but he also mentions as "Essential Ingredients of Happiness" the "Benevolent Affections," meaning "Love and Benevolence towards others," as well as "that Joy, which arises from their Happiness."<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="American_jurisprudence">American jurisprudence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: American jurisprudence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/U.S._Declaration_of_Independence" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Declaration of Independence">U.S. Declaration of Independence</a> states that it has become necessary for the people of the United States to assume "the separate and equal station to which the Laws of Nature and of Nature's God entitle them". Some early American lawyers and judges perceived natural law as too tenuous, amorphous, and evanescent a legal basis for grounding concrete <a href="/wiki/Rights" title="Rights">rights</a> and <a href="/wiki/Limited_government" title="Limited government">governmental limitations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Edlin_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edlin-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Natural law did, however, serve as authority for legal claims and rights in some judicial decisions, legislative acts, and legal pronouncements.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> Robert Lowry Clinton argues that the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Constitution">U.S. Constitution</a> rests on a <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> foundation and the common law, in turn, rests on a classical natural law foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_liberal_natural_law">European liberal natural law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: European liberal natural law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dr_Sir_Albericus_Gentilis_(Father_of_international_law).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Dr_Sir_Albericus_Gentilis_%28Father_of_international_law%29.png/190px-Dr_Sir_Albericus_Gentilis_%28Father_of_international_law%29.png" decoding="async" width="190" height="215" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/31/Dr_Sir_Albericus_Gentilis_%28Father_of_international_law%29.png 1.5x" data-file-width="243" data-file-height="275" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dr_Sir_Albericus_Gentilis_(Father_of_international_law).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alberico_Gentili" title="Alberico Gentili">Dr Alberico Gentili</a>, the founder of the science of international law.</div></div></div>
<p>Liberal natural law grew out of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval</a> Christian natural law theories and out of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Hobbes'</a> revision of natural law, sometimes in an uneasy balance of the two.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alberico_Gentili" title="Alberico Gentili">Sir Alberico Gentili</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Grotius" title="Hugo Grotius">Hugo Grotius</a> based their philosophies of international law on natural law. In particular, his writings on <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_seas" title="Freedom of the seas">freedom of the seas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Just_war_theory" title="Just war theory">just war theory</a> directly appealed to natural law. About natural law itself, he wrote that "even the will of an <a href="/wiki/Omnipotent" class="mw-redirect" title="Omnipotent">omnipotent</a> being cannot change or abrogate" natural law, which "would maintain its objective validity even if we should assume the impossible, that there is no <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> or that he does not care for human affairs." (<i><a href="/wiki/De_iure_belli_ac_pacis" class="mw-redirect" title="De iure belli ac pacis">De iure belli ac pacis</a></i>, Prolegomeni XI). This is the famous <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">argument</a> <i>etiamsi daremus</i> (<i>non esse Deum</i>), that made natural law no longer dependent on theology. However, German church-historians Ernst Wolf and M. Elze disagreed and claimed that Grotius' concept of natural law did have a theological basis.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In Grotius' view, the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> contained moral precepts (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Decalogue" class="mw-redirect" title="Decalogue">Decalogue</a>) which <a href="/wiki/Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Christ">Christ</a> confirmed and therefore were still valid. Moreover, they were useful in explaining the content of natural law. Both biblical revelation and natural law originated in God and could therefore not contradict each other.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In a similar way, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Pufendorf" class="mw-redirect" title="Samuel Pufendorf">Samuel Pufendorf</a> gave natural law a theological foundation and applied it to his concepts of government and <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> incorporated natural law into many of his theories and philosophy, especially in <i><a href="/wiki/Two_Treatises_of_Government" title="Two Treatises of Government">Two Treatises of Government</a></i>. There is considerable debate about whether his conception of natural law was more akin to that of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Aquinas</a> (filtered through <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hooker" title="Richard Hooker">Richard Hooker</a>) or <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Hobbes'</a> radical reinterpretation, though the effect of Locke's understanding is usually phrased in terms of a revision of Hobbes upon Hobbesian <a href="/wiki/Social_contract" title="Social contract">contractarian</a> grounds. Locke turned Hobbes' prescription around, saying that if the ruler went against natural law and failed to protect "life, liberty, and property," people could justifiably overthrow the existing state and create a new one.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>While Locke spoke in the language of natural law, the content of this law was by and large protective of <a href="/wiki/Natural_and_legal_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural and legal rights">natural rights</a>, and it was this language that later liberal thinkers preferred. Political philosopher <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Waldron" title="Jeremy Waldron">Jeremy Waldron</a> has pointed out that Locke's political thought was based on "a particular set of Protestant Christian assumptions."<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> To Locke, the content of natural law was identical with biblical ethics as laid down especially in the <a href="/wiki/Decalogue" class="mw-redirect" title="Decalogue">Decalogue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Christ">Christ</a>'s teaching and exemplary life, and St. Paul's admonitions.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Locke derived the concept of basic human equality, including the <a href="/wiki/Equality_of_the_sexes" class="mw-redirect" title="Equality of the sexes">equality of the sexes</a> ("Adam and Eve"), from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?">Genesis 1, 26–28</a>, the starting-point of the theological doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Imago_Dei" class="mw-redirect" title="Imago Dei">Imago Dei</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> One of the consequences is that as all humans are created equally free, governments need the consent of the governed.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a>, arguably echoing Locke, appealed to <a href="/wiki/Inalienable_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Inalienable rights">unalienable rights</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a></i>, "We hold these truths to be self-evident, that all men are <i>created</i> equal, that they are endowed by their <i>Creator</i> with certain unalienable Rights, that among these are <a href="/wiki/Life,_liberty_and_the_pursuit_of_happiness" class="mw-redirect" title="Life, liberty and the pursuit of happiness">Life, Liberty and the pursuit of Happiness</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The Lockean idea that governments need the <a href="/wiki/Consent_of_the_governed" title="Consent of the governed">consent of the governed</a> was also fundamental to the Declaration of Independence, as the American Revolutionaries used it as justification for their separation from the British crown.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Belgian philosopher of law <a href="/wiki/Frank_van_Dun" class="mw-redirect" title="Frank van Dun">Frank van Dun</a> is one among those who are elaborating a secular conception<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> of natural law in the liberal tradition. <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">Libertarian</a> theorist <a href="/wiki/Murray_Rothbard" title="Murray Rothbard">Murray Rothbard</a> argues that "the very existence of a natural law discoverable by reason is a potentially powerful threat to the status quo and a standing reproach to the reign of blindly traditional custom or the arbitrary will of the State apparatus."<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_von_Mises" title="Ludwig von Mises">Ludwig von Mises</a> states that he relaid the general sociological and economic foundations of the liberal doctrine upon utilitarianism, rather than natural law, but R. A. Gonce argues that "the reality of the argument constituting his system overwhelms his denial."<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Murray Rothbard, however, says that Gonce makes a lot of errors and distortions in the analysis of Mises's works, including making confusions about the term which Mises uses to refer to scientific laws, "laws of nature", saying it characterizes Mises as a natural law philosopher.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/David_Gordon_(philosopher)" title="David Gordon (philosopher)">David Gordon</a> notes, "When most people speak of natural law, what they have in mind is the contention that morality can be derived from human nature. If human beings are rational animals of such-and-such a sort, then the moral virtues are...(filling in the blanks is the difficult part)."<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Economist and philosopher <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hayek" title="Friedrich Hayek">F. A. Hayek</a> said that, originally, "the term 'natural' was used to describe an orderliness or regularity that was not the product of deliberate human will. Together with 'organism' it was one of the two terms generally understood to refer to the spontaneously grown in contrast to the invented or designed. Its use in this sense had been inherited from the stoic philosophy, had been revived in the twelfth century, and it was finally under its flag that the late Spanish Schoolmen developed the foundations of the genesis and functioning of spontaneously formed social institutions."<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The idea that 'natural' was "the product of designing reason" is a product of a seventeenth century <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalist</a> reinterpretation of the law of nature. <a href="/wiki/Luis_de_Molina" title="Luis de Molina">Luis Molina</a>, for example, when referred to the 'natural' price, explained that it is "so called because 'it results from the thing itself without regard to laws and decrees, but is dependent on many circumstances which alter it, such as the sentiments of men, their estimation of different uses, often even in consequence of whims and pleasures".<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> And even <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>, when talking about the foundations of natural law and explaining what he thought when citing "reason", said: "By reason, however, I do not think is meant here that faculty of the understanding which forms traint of thought and deduces proofs, but certain definite principles of action from which spring all virtues and whatever is necessary for the proper moulding of morals."<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This anti-rationalist approach to human affairs, for Hayek, was the same which guided <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Enlightenment" title="Scottish Enlightenment">Scottish enlightenment</a> thinkers, such as <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adam_Ferguson" title="Adam Ferguson">Adam Ferguson</a>, to make their case for liberty.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> For them, no one can have the knowledge necessary to plan society, and this "natural" or "spontaneous" order of society shows how it can efficiently "plan" bottom-up.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Also, the idea that law is just a product of deliberate design, denied by natural law and linked to <a href="/wiki/Legal_positivism" title="Legal positivism">legal positivism</a>, can easily generate <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">totalitarianism</a>: "If law is wholly the product of deliberate design, whatever the designer decrees to be law is just by definition and unjust law becomes a contradiction in terms. The will of the duly authorized legislator is then wholly unfettered and guided solely by his concrete interests".<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> This idea is wrong because law cannot be just a product of "reason": "no system of articulated law can be applied except within a framework of generally recognized but often unarticulated rules of justice".<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, a secular critique of the natural law doctrine was stated by <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Charron" title="Pierre Charron">Pierre Charron</a> in his <i>De la sagesse</i> (1601): "The sign of a natural law must be the universal respect in which it is held, for if there was anything that nature had truly commanded us to do, we would undoubtedly obey it universally: not only would every nation respect it, but every individual. Instead there is nothing in the world that is not subject to contradiction and dispute, nothing that is not rejected, not just by one nation, but by many; equally, there is nothing that is strange and (in the opinion of many) unnatural that is not approved in many countries, and authorized by their customs."
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_jurisprudence">Contemporary jurisprudence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Natural_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Contemporary jurisprudence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Jurisprudence" title="Jurisprudence">jurisprudence</a>, <i>natural law</i> can refer to the several doctrines:
</p>
<ul><li>That <a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">just laws</a> are <a href="/wiki/Immanence" title="Immanence">immanent</a> in nature; that is, they can be "discovered" or "found" but not "created" by such things as a <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_rights" title="Bill of rights">bill of rights</a>;</li>
<li>That they can emerge by the natural process of resolving conflicts, as embodied by the evolutionary process of the common law; or</li>
<li>That the meaning of law is such that its content cannot be determined except by reference to moral principles. These meanings can either oppose or complement each other, although they share the common trait that they rely on inherence as opposed to design in finding just laws.</li></ul>
<p>Whereas <a href="/wiki/Legal_positivism" title="Legal positivism">legal positivism</a> would say that a law can be unjust without it being any less a law, a natural law jurisprudence would say that there is something legally deficient about an unjust norm. <a href="/wiki/Legal_interpretivism" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal interpretivism">Legal interpretivism</a>, famously defended in the English-speaking world by <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Dworkin" title="Ronald Dworkin">Ronald Dworkin</a>, claims to have a position different from both natural law and positivism.
</p><p>Besides <a href="/wiki/Utilitarianism" title="Utilitarianism">utilitarianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kantianism" title="Kantianism">Kantianism</a>, natural law jurisprudence has in common with <a href="/wiki/Virtue_ethics" title="Virtue ethics">virtue ethics</a> that it is a live option for a <a href="/wiki/First_principles" class="mw-redirect" title="First principles">first principles</a> ethics theory in <a href="/wiki/Analytic_philosophy" title="Analytic philosophy">analytic philosophy</a>.
</p><p>The concept of natural law was very important in the development of the English <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a>. In the struggles between <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament</a> and the <a href="/wiki/British_monarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="British monarchy">monarch</a>, Parliament often made reference to the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_Laws_of_England" title="Fundamental Laws of England">Fundamental Laws of England</a>, which were at times said to embody natural law principles since <a href="/wiki/Time_immemorial" title="Time immemorial">time immemorial</a> and set limits on the power of the monarchy. According to <a href="/wiki/William_Blackstone" title="William Blackstone">William Blackstone</a>, however, natural law might be useful in determining the content of the common law and in deciding cases of <a href="/wiki/Equity_(law)" title="Equity (law)">equity</a>, but was not itself identical with the laws of England. Nonetheless, the implication of natural law in the common law tradition has meant that the great opponents of natural law and advocates of <a href="/wiki/Legal_positivism" title="Legal positivism">legal positivism</a>, like <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Bentham" title="Jeremy Bentham">Jeremy Bentham</a>, have also been staunch critics of the common law.
</p><p>Natural law jurisprudence is currently undergoing a period of reformulation (as is legal positivism). The most prominent contemporary natural law jurist, Australian <a href="/wiki/John_Finnis" title="John Finnis">John Finnis</a>, is based in Oxford, but there are also Americans <a href="/wiki/Germain_Grisez" title="Germain Grisez">Germain Grisez</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_P._George" title="Robert P. George">Robert P. George</a>, and Canadian <a href="/w/index.php?title=Joseph_Boyle_(jurist)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Joseph Boyle (jurist) (page does not exist)">Joseph Boyle</a> and Brazilian Emídio Brasileiro. All have tried to construct a new version of natural law. The 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchist</a> and legal theorist, <a href="/wiki/Lysander_Spooner" title="Lysander Spooner">Lysander Spooner</a>, was also a figure in the expression of modern natural law.
</p><p>"New Natural Law" as it is sometimes called, originated with Grisez. It focuses on "basic human goods", such as human life, knowledge, and aesthetic experience, which are <a href="/wiki/Self-evidence" title="Self-evidence">self-evidently</a> and intrinsically worthwhile, and states that these goods reveal themselves as being <a href="/wiki/Commensurability_(ethics)" title="Commensurability (ethics)">incommensurable</a> with one another.
</p><p>The tensions between the natural law and the positive law have played, and continue to play a key role in the development of <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> wrote to <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> in 1826 that before the Revolution, the first volume of Coke's <i>Institutes of the Laws of England</i> "was the universal elementary book of law students, and a sounder Whig never wrote, nor of profounder learning in the orthodox doctrines of the British constitution, or in what were called English liberties."
